---
Page 10
Line > 1 Both the state of Oklahoma and the defendant,
Line > 2 Mr. Norton, are entitled to jurors who approach this case with
Line > 3 open minds and agree to keep their minds open until a verdict
Line > 4 is reached. Jurors should be as free as humanly possible from
Line > 5 any bias, prejudice or sympathy. Jurors must not be influenced
Line > 6 by preconceived ideas as to the facts or to the law.
Line > 7 From this point until the conclusion of the trial, I’m
Line > 8 going to ask you not to discuss this case with any person,
Line > 9 including family or friends. I’m going to ask you not to read
Line > 10 or listen to any media discussing this case, nor research this
Line > 11 case in any way including through the internet or other tools
Line > 12 of technology. Nor should you use any of these means to
Line > 13 communicate to others about this case. It is important that
Line > 14 this case be decided solely upon the evidence that you receive
Line > 15 in this courtroom.
Line > 16 You’re all undoubtedly qualified to serve as jurors,
Line > 17 but you may not be qualified to serve as a juror in this
Line > 18 particular case. So the law permits unlimited challenges for
Line > 19 cause. Also, the law grants both the State and the defendant
Line > 20 five peremptory challenges.
Line > 21 A peremptory challenge permits either the State or the
Line > 22 defendant to excuse a prospective juror for any reason allowed
Line > 23 by law. If you’re excused from being a juror in this
Line > 24 particular case, it’s not a reflection on you. You may well be
Line > 25 better suited to serve as a juror in a different case.

Line > 13 the case of the State of Oklahoma versus Dallas Norton.
Line > 14 As I’ve told you before, the defendant has pled not
Line > 15 guilty. A plea of not guilty puts at issue each element of the
Line > 16 crimes with which the defendant is charged.
Line > 17 A plea of guilty requires the state to prove each
Line > 18 element of the crimes beyond a reasonable doubt.
Line > 19 The defendant is presumed innocent of the crimes and the
Line > 20 presumption continues unless after consideration of all the
Line > 21 evidence you are convinced of his guilt beyond a reasonable
Line > 22 doubt.
Line > 23 The State has the burden of presenting the evidence
Line > 24 that establishes guilt beyond a reasonable doubt. The
Line > 25 defendant must be found not guilty unless the state produces
---
Page 236
Line > 1 evidence which convinces you beyond a reasonable doubt of each
Line > 2 element of the crimes.
Line > 3 I’m also going to give you a pad and a pencil so that
Line > 4 you can take notes. Those will be on your chairs when you
Line > 5 return tomorrow morning. I want you to remember these things
Line > 6 about taking notes.
Line > 7 First, that note taking is permitted but it’s not
Line > 8 required. Also, take notes sparingly. Don’t try to write down
Line > 9 all the testimony. Your notes will only be used for the
Line > 10 purpose of refreshing your memory. TheyTre helpful in dealing
Line > 11 with things like measurements, times, distances, identities,
Line > 12 relationships.
Line > 13 Third, I encourage you to be brief in your note
Line > 14 taking. It’s for you to determine the credibility of the
Line > 15 witnesses. And to do so, you need to observe them. So don’t
Line > 16 let note taking be a distraction from this duty.
Line > 17 Finally, your notes are for your private use. Don’t
Line > 18 share your notes with any other juror during the presentation
Line > 19 of the case. You may discuss the contents of your notes only
Line > 20 after all sides have rested and you have commenced your
Line > 21 deliberation. In other words, you’ll be allowed to take your
Line > 22 notes with your to the deliberation room.
